      <li id="YG29">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="6h6N">
        <p>Hilarity to it all</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="mOQj">
            <p>I had this quite beautiful concept I used to actively practice.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="OR">
            <p>Admittatedly I still practice it, although recently I've neglected it</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4O">
            <p>a little. I used to imagine someone, a passerby, on the street, a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="qe">
            <p>total stranger, I used to imagine their hug and their embrace. After</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-7">
            <p>all, they've definitely had at least one embrace in their lifetime,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="TW">
            <p>right? And this thought experiment is actually quite easy to apply.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Zx">
            <p>It's not hard to imagine hugging someone. In theory it should connect</p>
          </li>
          <li id="LA">
            <p>you better with them.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="6eMD">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="3j">
            <p>Today I'm thinking about this thought experiment, but in applying it</p>
          </li>
          <li id="FH">
            <p>to a different degree. Everything is a fucking clown. Imagine it.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="NT">
            <p>Everything is one big fucking joke. Imagine these fuckers, what do we</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5H">
            <p>toil for daily? No-one will actually remember you. You'll be dead and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Rb">
            <p>gone in a hundred years and no-one will even notice. Those that do</p>
          </li>
          <li id="__">
            <p>will forget quickly. The whole thing is one big game. I imagine</p>
          </li>
          <li id="HL">
            <p>someone somewhere is laughing about it all.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="mb7w">
        <p/>
      </li>

      <li id="ntoB">
        <p>Magic Keyboard</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="Y9">
            <p>I bought these expensive keyboards, but they're pretty useless to me.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="w6">
            <p>I don't know, the extra travel on keypresses makes me feel slow. I</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wo">
            <p>feel like I can move quickly on a Magic Keyboard.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="b8">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="0d">
            <p>This is probably because I grew up using Magic keyboards instead of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Rq">
            <p>mechanical keyboards? Magic &gt; mechanical for me, as much as I hate to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ng">
            <p>admit it.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ES">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="7t">
            <p>Sometimes you have to buy the expensive thing to realise the cheap</p>
          </li>
          <li id="w5Ag">
            <p>thing is actually better **shrug**.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tw">

          </li>

        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="LA7S">
        <p>Quality of Life</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="FxUo">
            <p>In my opinion, this is a really nice quote and applicable to all software:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5G">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="7p">
            <p>&gt; Quality of life versus flashy features  I know that you are</p>
          </li>
          <li id="EV">
            <p>&gt; mainly looking forward to the new content, and that just quality of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="W2">
            <p>&gt; life improvements aren't the kind of things that make people buy the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tR">
            <p>&gt; game and get excited for.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="S3">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7MKB">
            <p>&gt; But I strongly believe, that if you want to add content, mechanics,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="M4RI">
            <p>&gt; and systems to a game, which already isn't simple, there is always a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="aA4G">
            <p>&gt; risk of just it being too much. By doing QOL improvements, we reduce</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7Z">
            <p>&gt; the small hassles and annoyances, which effectively creates an extra</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-o">
            <p>&gt; mental space to enjoy more in the game. Its like cleaning your room</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1H">
            <p>&gt; before getting a new toy.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ui">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ip">
            <p>&gt; So to make things clear, the reason that we make and present these</p>
          </li>
          <li id="jt">
            <p>&gt; kind of changes is not because we don't want to make new flashy</p>
          </li>
          <li id="dx">
            <p>&gt; features, we just want the new stuff to be enjoyable without a burden</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tq">
            <p>&gt; of having too much to deal with. But don't worry, we will show</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_x">
            <p>&gt; something new next week!</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_r">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="I0">
            <p>Michal Kovařík</p>
          </li>
          <li id="PI">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="d9">
            <p>Link: &lt;https://factorio.com/blog/post/fff-374&gt;</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="ZvLL">

      <li id="Anfn">
        <p>Sleepy Waiter</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="KrUn">
            <p>&gt; A sleepy waiter took my order for lemonade and melted cheese on toast.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="i7">
            <p>&gt; He wrote down the order with a stubby pencil, in no particular hurry.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Cc">
            <p>&gt; Sweat had seeped through the back of his shirt, forming a large stain.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="cf">
            <p>&gt; The stain seemed to be sending a message but I couldn't decipher it.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1s">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Ds">
            <p>&gt; Lonely metal souls in the unimpeded darkness of space, they meet, pass</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-5">
            <p>&gt; eachother, and part, never to meet again. No words passing between</p>
          </li>
          <li id="sYEX">
            <p>&gt; them, no promises to keep.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9LL4">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="--">
            <p>&gt; Like you're on a train at night travelling across some vast plain and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="18">
            <p>&gt; you catch a glimpse of a tiny light in the window of a farmhouse. In</p>
          </li>
          <li id="fALO">
            <p>&gt; an instant it's sucked back into the darkness behind and vanishes. But</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tI">
            <p>&gt; if you close your eyes that point of light stays with you, just</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ym">
            <p>&gt; barely, for a few moments.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="ZeTO">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="137Y">
        <p>Delegate Understanding</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="8bLB">
            <p>&gt; There is a parasite, I see it everywhere. It consumes your health and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="xZ">
            <p>&gt; wealth. It preys on ignorance and is easy to catch. It’s so common you</p>
          </li>
          <li id="K7">
            <p>&gt; may not even notice you have it.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Uy">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Pr">
            <p>&gt; The parasite has a simple and attractive proposition: let me take care</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ba">
            <p>&gt; of this hard thing for you. Trust me, I know better.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="SZ">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="z5-z">
            <p>&gt; Instead of understanding it yourself, you choose to give the parasite</p>
          </li>
          <li id="OT">
            <p>&gt; control over your health, education, money, housing, business,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="i0Kl">
            <p>&gt; identity, data, infrastructure, climate, justice. Even your beliefs.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Nz">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nV">
            <p>&gt; The parasite has three stages: acceptance, extraction, intervention.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="VI">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1sIc">
            <p>&gt; First is acceptance. Everyone else seems to have the parasite already.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ia">
            <p>&gt; You are expected, even encouraged, to accept the parasite into your</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ruma">
            <p>&gt; life. You are invited to follow the norm, outsource, consume. It’s</p>
          </li>
          <li id="iX">
            <p>&gt; okay! Use all the services and amenities. Satisfy your desires. Eat</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WQ">
            <p>&gt; the cheap food, watch the cheap media. Your money and time are meant</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Gh">
            <p>&gt; to be spent. Show off what you got in exchange. Please do not try to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1PYZ">
            <p>&gt; understand how it works, it’s too complicated for you. The parasite</p>
          </li>
          <li id="gV">
            <p>&gt; wants you fattened. Literally and figuratively. You are paying the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="TL">
            <p>&gt; parasite for the privilege of being ripened.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="gw">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5iyN">
            <p>&gt; Second is extraction. Under the influence of the parasite, you have</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tO">
            <p>&gt; developed unhealthy habits and you are suffering the consequences.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="bd">
            <p>&gt; Stress, anxiety, obesity, disease, fear, lethargy, decay. To dampen</p>
          </li>
          <li id="w9bS">
            <p>&gt; these problems you pay the parasite for help — support, medicine,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Tr">
            <p>&gt; loans, fines, rent, taxes. Enforcement of some homeostasis. You try to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8J">
            <p>&gt; abate the issues, but you don’t have a stable foundation to build on.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="PWQu">
            <p>&gt; You have ignored the root causes. The parasite thrives. You are paying</p>
          </li>
          <li id="e6">
            <p>&gt; the parasite to be harvested, milked, sucked dry.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="un">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2s">
            <p>&gt; Third is intervention. The side effects of the parasite’s extraction</p>
          </li>
          <li id="iN5o">
            <p>&gt; have reached a critical level. The parasite tells you it’s an</p>
          </li>
          <li id="6T">
            <p>&gt; emergency. You need doctors, lawyers, firefighters, a military effort.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2vN0">
            <p>&gt; You’re in a surgery room, a court room, a psychiatric ward, a jail</p>
          </li>
          <li id="M9">
            <p>&gt; cell. The disease can no longer be controlled, it has festered. The</p>
          </li>
          <li id="O3v9">
            <p>&gt; flame has turned into a raging fire that needs to be put out. You are</p>
          </li>
          <li id="oc">
            <p>&gt; paying the parasite to go back to square one.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="k2">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Km">
            <p>&gt; The three stages of the parasite are interdependent. Every stage</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nfCx">
            <p>&gt; benefits someone who is not you. Everyone tells you this is just the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="CmvD">
            <p>&gt; way it is. Never mind that the parasite is living large.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="r0">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="lxQ">
            <p>&gt; Why? Extraction and intervention pay well. Education and prevention do</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3si">
            <p>&gt; not. The incentives are aligned to make the parasite persuasive. You</p>
          </li>
          <li id="xH-">
            <p>&gt; are alone against a coordinated system that is exceedingly effective</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9Za">
            <p>&gt; at packaging problems you should never have with solutions you should</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9Wp">
            <p>&gt; never need. A symbiotic loop.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="atT">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Zur">
            <p>&gt; You must recognize the parasite in its earliest form.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="BlM">
            <p>&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="jej">
            <p>&gt; To inoculate yourself don’t delegate understanding. If you build your</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2ij">
            <p>&gt; own understanding you will be the one who earns the dividends.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="lzq">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="k-4">
            <p>&lt;https://stephanango.com/understand&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ykQS">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="jXbL">
        <p>Email from John Sculley, 1990</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="UG">
            <p>Email from General Magic co-founder Marc Porat to then- Apple CEO John</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8j">
            <p>Sculley in 1990:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WG">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="oodE">
            <p>&gt; “A tiny computer, a phone, a very personal object . . . It must be</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Lk">
            <p>&gt; beautiful. It must offer the kind of personal satisfaction that a fine</p>
          </li>
          <li id="0r">
            <p>&gt; piece of jewellery brings. It will have a perceived value even when it’s</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ok">
            <p>&gt; not being used. It will offer the comfort of a touchstone, the tactile</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nC">
            <p>&gt; satisfaction of a seashell, the enchantment of a crystal. Once you use</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Pu">
            <p>&gt; it you won’t be able to live without it.”</p>
          </li>
          <li id="BSRp">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>

        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="srxy">
        <p>You have to go all in</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="1M">
            <p>You begin to notice that the people who are exceptional, and I mean by</p>
          </li>
          <li id="VI-s">
            <p>this the people that have the "[creme de la creme](20221229T191107--first-person-singular.md)", have worked</p>
          </li>
          <li id="LI">
            <p>incredibly hard. They've put in the time and effort.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="6L">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="20">
            <p>You don't become exceptional by doing the minimum. You don't become</p>
          </li>
          <li id="N3">
            <p>exceptional by sitting on the fence.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="co">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="mu">
            <p>You have to go all in.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="a8OY">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>

      <li id="Qh3w">
        <p>People ask me why I use Emacs</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="P6lh">
            <p>I don't use Emacs because it's a better editor.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mM">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="5PWi">
            <p>I don't use Emacs to look cool.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="B1Vi">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="atro">
            <p>I don't use Emacs because it's open software.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="D8">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="lgVS">
            <p>I use Emacs because the people I've observed using Emacs have</p>
          </li>
          <li id="uk83">
            <p>*character*. Yeah they're not perfect. Who is? At least they have some</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mG">
            <p>soul. Some conviction.</p>
          </li>

      <li id="ds8P">
        <p>Interaction design</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="K5iT">
            <p>So, I know I'm pretty good at programming.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="59">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="hF">
            <p>I know I'm pretty good at design.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="kq">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="D2">
            <p>But that only seems to be 50% of this problem (Amie application).</p>
          </li>
          <li id="C4Ls">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="ya">
            <p>I also need to account for good interaction design. This challegne is</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9a">
            <p>the gift that just keeps on giving.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="CDor">

      <li id="fOhc">
        <p>Nuggets of knowledge from Nat Hayward</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="LbmK">
            <p>My first design lead and boss who I respect a lot. She taught me two important concepts:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8OVy">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="6UtT">
            <p>1.  In the concept phase, ugly is better. Use thick strokes. Intentionally apply constraints.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="od">
            <p>2.  Your 70% is most people's 100% (in terms of design).</p>
          </li>
          <li id="59G9">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="0E-q">
        <p>Make a generation stupid at scale</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="mk">
            <p>This:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="29">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="g-">
            <p>How to gauge how stupid the general public are on any given day. Open</p>
          </li>
          <li id="0z">
            <p>YT in a private window and look at the recommended feed. It</p>
          </li>
          <li id="vr">
            <p>disappoints and depresses me every single time. Without fail.</p>
          </li>

            <p/>
          </li>

          </li>

          </li>

        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="coFC">
        <p>Everytime someone messages you</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="bY">
            <p>Every time someone messages you, there should be a minimum time before</p>
          </li>
          <li id="gN">
            <p>you produce a response, something like 5 minutes.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="inbc">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="8-4g">
        <p>Lorem Ipsum is dangerous</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="te">
            <p>I've always thought that lorem ipsum in design is pretty dangerous.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="AM">
            <p>What are you going to fill that text with? If you don't know exactly</p>
          </li>
          <li id="RmTZ">
            <p>what you're going to fill that space with, you're over designing.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="_HNR">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="4XUq">
        <p>Swift action</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="4jfM">
            <p>You and your whole team should always try to take swift action and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="lw">
            <p>make progress each day. Instead of thinking or talking about doing</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nG">
            <p>something, you decide to do it or not to do it. Then you do it today</p>
          </li>
          <li id="EQ">
            <p>instead of tomorrow and this week instead of next week.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_6">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="1O">
            <p>There will be also weeks when you won’t necessarily know what is the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-_Qr">
            <p>most important thing to do or you are not sure what decision to make</p>
          </li>
          <li id="qM">
            <p>in the product. Don’t become paralyzed in those moments–find a way to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Z2">
            <p>act instead. Trust your intuition and do something that seems to make</p>
          </li>
          <li id="gk">
            <p>sense. Talk to more users. You’ll gain more clarity as more feedback</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Mt">
            <p>rolls in. If you’ve designed your operations to move fast and learn,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Vb">
            <p>then you can correct or revert decisions.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Nf">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="hRxu">
            <p>Startups rarely die because they made too much progress or because of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ADOa">
            <p>a single bad decision, but they do die when they move too slow or give</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Tx">
            <p>up.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zY">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Bs7u">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>

            <p>“The goal was never to beat the competition, or to make a lot of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="jJ">
            <p>money. It was to do the greatest thing possible or even a little</p>
          </li>
          <li id="pL">
            <p>greater”</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Us">
            <p/>
          </li>

      <li id="86nd">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="3tLz">
        <p>Bauhaus</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="AX3-">
            <p>Alright, imagine you're a kid and you have a big box of building</p>
          </li>
          <li id="EY">
            <p>blocks. Some blocks are red, some are blue, and some are yellow. Now,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2v">
            <p>instead of building fancy and ornate castles with intricate designs,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="qi">
            <p>you decide to make simple, functional structures with clean lines and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8i">
            <p>primary colors. You believe that these simple structures, even though</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tD">
            <p>they're not fancy, are beautiful in their own way because of their</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4DDJ">
            <p>simplicity, function, and the basic colors you're using.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="cl">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="vg">
            <p>This idea of simple, functional, and clean design is a lot like the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="conR">
            <p>Bauhaus movement.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="JV">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="DiHq">
            <p>Back in the day, around the 1920s in Germany, a school called the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8yo7">
            <p>Bauhaus was founded. This school believed that art, crafts, and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="v3rU">
            <p>technology should come together to create functional and simple</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Hx">
            <p>designs. They thought that objects, even everyday ones like chairs or</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Pn">
            <p>teapots, should be both useful and beautiful in a simple way.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="kC">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="xn">
            <p>Instead of adding lots of decoration and details to objects and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nGxZ">
            <p>buildings, Bauhaus designers kept things straightforward and focused</p>
          </li>
          <li id="19">
            <p>on the basic forms and functions. They also loved using primary</p>
          </li>
          <li id="io">
            <p>colors, just like your building blocks.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9R">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="ci0V">
            <p>So, in short, the Bauhaus movement was all about simplicity,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="cBUr">
            <p>functionality, and bringing different arts together to make everyday</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ZhnA">
            <p>things beautiful in their own unique way.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Tu">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="gY2h">
            <p>TODO: buy the book</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Br">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="iI84">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="35aa">
        <p>Even what is hidden should remain beautiful</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="7OlE">
            <p>“I want it to be as beautiful as possible, even if it’s inside the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Dr">
            <p>box. A great carpenter isn’t going to use lousy wood for the back of a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="aS">
            <p>cabinet, even though nobody’s going to see it”</p>
          </li>
          <li id="It">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="oTdw">
            <p>Steve Jobs</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>

      <li id="_6_Q">
        <p>Ives and Rams</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="pg16">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="If">
            <p>Ive was a fan of the German industrial designer Dieter Rams, who</p>
          </li>
          <li id="vb">
            <p>worked for the electronics firm Braun. Rams preached the gospel of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="CVHC">
            <p>“Less but better”, Weniger aber besser, and likewise Jobs and Ive</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tm">
            <p>wrestled with each new design to see how much they could simplify it.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1N">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="1p4t">
            <p>Jobs Book</p>
          </li>
        </ul>

      <li id="Qr87">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="ApoF">
        <p>Why is Simple good?</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="LRJ2">
            <p>Why do we assume that simple is good? Because with physical products,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zD">
            <p>we have to feel we can dominate them. As you bring order to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="p_">
            <p>complexity, you find a way to make the product defer to you.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nR">
            <p>Simplicity isn’t just a visual style. It’s not just minimalism or the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="korh">
            <p>absence of clutter. It involves digging through the depth of the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="YO">
            <p>complexity. To be truly simple, you have to go really deep.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="UK">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Zg">
            <p>Jobs</p>
          </li>
          <li id="dTg1">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="HaJ9">
        <p>Ive and Jobs, knife</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="AsAf">
            <p>Ive picked up a knife he admired, but then put it down in</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4Gc4">
            <p>disappointment. Jobs did the same. “We both noticed a tiny bit of glue</p>
          </li>
          <li id="hZ">
            <p>between the handle and the blade”, Ive recalled. They talked about how</p>
          </li>
          <li id="r1hn">
            <p>how the knife’s hold design had been ruined by the way it was</p>
          </li>
          <li id="QR">
            <p>manufactured. “We don’t like to think of our knives as being glued</p>
          </li>
          <li id="pJsT">
            <p>together”, Ive said. “Steve and I care about things like that, which</p>
          </li>
          <li id="eV">
            <p>ruin the purity and detract from the essence of something like a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="goaz">
            <p>toenail, and we think alike about how products should be made to look</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Mbqi">
            <p>pure and seamless”</p>
          </li>
          <li id="38">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="To">
            <p>Steve Jobs Book</p>
          </li>
          <li id="c5Mv">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="1qFT">
        <p>Mind Pollution</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="MdxD">
            <p>I really do believe there is this idea that your mind can be polluted</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tV">
            <p>by the bullshit around you. You should actively seek to purge it and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="P9">
            <p>block it from your life. E.g. most mainstream adverts aimed at boring</p>
          </li>
          <li id="pa">
            <p>and worthless laypeople, or TikTok videos.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="c8P4">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="DVmh">
        <p>To All Conspiracy Theorists</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="oYM1">
            <p>To all conspiracy theorists: I took a 5 year degree in computing. When</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3l">
            <p>I finished my degree, I had more questions than when I started. I</p>
          </li>
          <li id="S8">
            <p>encourage you to do the same, and then you will realise that it is an</p>
          </li>
          <li id="slYu">
            <p>impossible task to take a hardline stance on anything, especially in</p>
          </li>
          <li id="yc">
            <p>such a short window of time as a day, or a week.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>

            <p>`Package-vc-install`</p>

          <li id="jT">
            <p/>
            <ul>
              <li id="sy">
                <p>;; NOTE 2022-11-20: This may not cover every case, though it works</p>
              </li>
              <li id="mN">
                <p>;; fine in my workflow.  I am still undecided by EWW.</p>
              </li>
              <li id="1q">
                <p>(defun prot/enable-variable-pitch ()</p>
              </li>
              <li id="tZ">
                <p>(unless (or (derived-mode-p 'mhtml-mode 'nxml-mode 'yaml-mode)</p>
                <ul>
                  <li id="Ua">
                    <p>(member (buffer-name) '("*Colors*" "*Faces*" "*Quick Help*")))</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="JL">
                    <p>(variable-pitch-mode 1)))</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="ZC">
                    <p/>
                  </li>
                </ul>
              </li>
              <li id="hd">
                <p>(defvar prot/enable-variable-pitch-in-hooks</p>
              </li>
              <li id="M4">
                <p>'(text-mode-hook</p>
                <ul>
                  <li id="EsMm">
                    <p>help-mode-hook</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="V4mX">
                    <p>notmuch-show-mode-hook</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="z2">
                    <p>elfeed-show-mode-hook)</p>
                  </li>
                </ul>
              </li>
              <li id="0L">
                <p>"List of hook symbols to add `prot/enable-variable-pitch' to.")</p>
              </li>
              <li id="W5">
                <p/>
              </li>
              <li id="LD">
                <p>(dolist (hook prot/enable-variable-pitch-in-hooks)</p>
              </li>
              <li id="wv">
                <p>(add-hook hook #'prot/enable-variable-pitch))</p>
              </li>
              <li id="Zp">
                <p/>
              </li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li id="Hj">
            <p>`text-mode-hook` also works for org-mode</p>
          </li>

          </li>

            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="HeAH">
        <p>Suckless software principles</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="7X">
            <p>Philosophy</p>
          </li>
          <li id="0V">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="z_">
            <p>We are the home of quality software such as dwm, dmenu, st and plenty</p>
          </li>
          <li id="dU">
            <p>of other tools, with a focus on simplicity, clarity and frugality. Our</p>
          </li>
          <li id="UP">
            <p>philosophy is about keeping things simple, minimal and usable. We</p>
          </li>
          <li id="jA">
            <p>believe this should become the mainstream philosophy in the IT</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-V">
            <p>sector. Unfortunately, the tendency for complex, error-prone and slow</p>
          </li>
          <li id="A4">
            <p>software seems to be prevalent in the present-day software</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ns">
            <p>industry. We intend to prove the opposite with our software projects.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="6a">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="_p">
            <p>Our project focuses on advanced and experienced computer users. In</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ox">
            <p>contrast with the usual proprietary software world or many mainstream</p>
          </li>
          <li id="sFG0">
            <p>open source projects that focus more on average and non-technical end</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Vi">
            <p>users, we think that experienced users are mostly ignored. This is</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7a">
            <p>particularly true for user interfaces, such as graphical environments</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WL">
            <p>on desktop computer, on mobile devices, and in so-called Web</p>
          </li>
          <li id="xP">
            <p>applications. We believe that the market of experienced users is</p>
          </li>
          <li id="xV">
            <p>growing continuously, with each user looking for more appropriate</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7n">
            <p>solutions for his/her work style.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="HA">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="W6">
            <p>Designing simple and elegant software is far more difficult than</p>
          </li>
          <li id="bQ">
            <p>letting ad-hoc or over-ambitious features obscure the code over</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Er">
            <p>time. However one has to pay this price to achieve reliability and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="NM">
            <p>maintainability. Furthermore, minimalism results in reasonable and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zZ">
            <p>attainable goals. We strive to maintain minimalism and clarity to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="IZ">
            <p>drive development to completion.  Manifest</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ag">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="iL">
            <p>Many (open source) hackers are proud if they achieve large amounts of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="NJXE">
            <p>code, because they believe the more lines of code they've written, the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="G_">
            <p>more progress they have made. The more progress they have made, the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="E8">
            <p>more skilled they are. This is simply a delusion.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Q5">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="G8">
            <p>Most hackers actually don't care much about code quality. Thus, if</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Rd">
            <p>they get something working which seems to solve a problem, they stick</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ov">
            <p>with it. If this kind of software development is applied to the same</p>
          </li>
          <li id="SI">
            <p>source code throughout its entire life-cycle, we're left with large</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Cx">
            <p>amounts of code, a totally screwed code structure, and a flawed system</p>
          </li>
          <li id="t8">
            <p>design. This is because of a lack of conceptual clarity and integrity</p>
          </li>
          <li id="sY">
            <p>in the development process.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3x">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="uZ">
            <p>Code complexity is the mother of bloated, hard to use, and totally</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Si">
            <p>inconsistent software. With complex code, problems are solved in</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_Z">
            <p>suboptimal ways, valuable resources are endlessly tied up, performance</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ak">
            <p>slows to a halt, and vulnerabilities become a commonplace. The only</p>
          </li>
          <li id="QD">
            <p>solution is to scrap the entire project and rewrite it from scratch.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="FE">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="qG">
            <p>The bad news: quality rewrites rarely happen, because hackers are</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nU">
            <p>proud of large amounts of code. They think they understand the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="hK">
            <p>complexity in the code, thus there's no need to rewrite it. They think</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Gv">
            <p>of themselves as masterminds, understanding what others can never hope</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8s">
            <p>to grasp. To these types, complex software is the ideal.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="BE8">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Squ">
            <p>Ingenious ideas are simple. Ingenious software is simple. Simplicity</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Jxt">
            <p>is the heart of the Unix philosophy. The more code lines you have</p>
          </li>
          <li id="0Xb">
            <p>removed, the more progress you have made. As the number of lines of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5qf">
            <p>code in your software shrinks, the more skilled you have become and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="UYZ">
            <p>the less your software sucks.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-9N">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="qgg">
            <p>&lt;https://suckless.org/philosophy/&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wTYG">
            <p/>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="amdI">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="ggQy">
        <p>Ghibli wallpaper collection</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="KX">
            <p>&lt;https://drive.google.com/drive/folders/1MPECo6qYJejKuYdIot6EgvfINvAPnTGv&gt;</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="YXH_">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="jCCT">
        <p>Visual design rules you can safely follow</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="rrc0">
            <p>&lt;https://anthonyhobday.com/sideprojects/saferules/&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="GH">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="69">
            <p>Visual design rules you can safely follow every time</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mx">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Oy">
            <p>You do not have to follow these rules every time. If you have a good</p>
          </li>
          <li id="LR">
            <p>reason to break any of them, do. But they are safe to follow every</p>
          </li>
          <li id="s3">
            <p>time.  Use near-black and near-white instead of pure black and white</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wG">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="4K">
            <p>Pure black looks unnatural on a screen, and pure white is too</p>
          </li>
          <li id="KT">
            <p>bright. Use close-to-black and close-to-white instead. Any other</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mr">
            <p>references to “black” and “white” in these rules assume you’re</p>
          </li>
          <li id="eL5B">
            <p>following this rule.  Saturate your neutrals</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Dq">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Nx">
            <p>A neutral is generally a black, white, or grey. If you use colour in</p>
          </li>
          <li id="sR">
            <p>your interface, add a little bit of that colour to your neutrals. This</p>
          </li>
          <li id="W-">
            <p>will make the colour palette feel more coherent. If you use the HSB</p>
          </li>
          <li id="e1">
            <p>colour system less than 5% saturation should do it.  Use high contrast</p>
          </li>
          <li id="eOKf">
            <p>for important elements</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Qx">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="V_">
            <p>Important elements means buttons, content, or anything else that the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="FX">
            <p>user needs to notice. A higher contrast means that the element will</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Qm">
            <p>grab attention, which is useful for important elements. Elements that</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Bd">
            <p>the user does not need to notice (e.g. structural elements,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="b_">
            <p>drop-shadows) can use as little contrast as possible.  Everything in</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wk">
            <p>your design should be deliberate</p>
          </li>
          <li id="XJ">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="uv">
            <p>You should be deliberate about absolutely everything in your</p>
          </li>
          <li id="HR">
            <p>design. This means whitespace, alignment, size, spacing, colour,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="K4">
            <p>shadows. Everything. If someone points at a random part of your design</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WX">
            <p>you should have an explanation for why it looks that way. If you do</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tQ">
            <p>not do this your design will not feel coherent.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Oi">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="RF">
            <p>If you are new to design you can use this rule as a prompt to learn</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Qo">
            <p>more about what you are not deliberate about yet.  Optical alignment</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-a">
            <p>is often better than mathematical alignment</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ds">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="78">
            <p>Your design software can align things mathematically. But some shapes</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Eb">
            <p>don’t suit this type of alignment. For example, some odd shapes have a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="gz">
            <p>visual centre that is different from their mathematical centre. Often</p>
          </li>
          <li id="BT">
            <p>you will need to align things by eye so that it looks right.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="C-">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="fH">
            <p>It takes some practice to be able to align things by eye, but if you</p>
          </li>
          <li id="um">
            <p>do it regularly you’ll quickly pick it up.  Lower letter spacing and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="LH">
            <p>line height with larger text. Raise them with smaller text</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Gk">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="D-">
            <p>This applies to all text. The bigger the text, the less space you need</p>
          </li>
          <li id="u1">
            <p>between each letter and each line. The reverse is also true. If you do</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Dy">
            <p>not do this large text can look spread out, and small text can look</p>
          </li>
          <li id="A8">
            <p>too close.  Container borders should contrast with both the container</p>
          </li>
          <li id="hq">
            <p>and the background</p>
          </li>
          <li id="V5">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="24">
            <p>Example: If you have a card with a 1px border and a dark background,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="rk">
            <p>and it sits on top of an even darker background, the 1px border should</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WO">
            <p>be lighter than both of them. It should not be set to a brightness</p>
          </li>
          <li id="e9">
            <p>somewhere between the card and page background colours. Otherwise the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1Y">
            <p>edge of the container won’t look sharp enough. The same applies to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wF">
            <p>light background colours: the 1px border should be darker than both</p>
          </li>
          <li id="pT">
            <p>background colours.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ji">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Xj">
            <p>In the example below the left side is incorrect and the right side is</p>
          </li>
          <li id="QxIp">
            <p>correct.  Everything should be aligned with something else</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zn">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="emE0">
            <p>Alignment helps us realise that things are related to each other. If</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8h">
            <p>something is not aligned with anything else, it feels like it does not</p>
          </li>
          <li id="iM">
            <p>belong in the design. Ideally each element should be aligned with</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4ZHU">
            <p>other elements based on some kind of logic.  Colours in a palette</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7y">
            <p>should have distinct brightness values</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Uu">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="_I">
            <p>When colours have different brightness values, this helps them look</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mi">
            <p>and feel distinctive not just in hue, but in brightness. This leads to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="fl">
            <p>better colour palettes because colours don’t compete with each other</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ys">
            <p>as much.  If you saturate your neutrals you should use warm or cool</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Mr">
            <p>colours, not both</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Lh">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="neB">
            <p>If you use both warm and cool colours to saturate neutrals, the colour</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5ij">
            <p>palette will not feel coherent.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="a-W">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="tf7">
            <p>In the example below the left side uses a warm background and a cool</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1MS">
            <p>foreground. The right side uses a warm background and foreground.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="NAK">
            <p>Measurements should be mathematically related</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Hzz">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="iMY">
            <p>The spacing you use between elements, and the size of elements, should</p>
          </li>
          <li id="GEx">
            <p>be determined by some kind of scale. This will help the design to look</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Nd3">
            <p>coherent. In the example below, every element uses multiples</p>
          </li>
          <li id="e4i">
            <p>of 8. Horizontal and vertical grids based on a scale help if you want</p>
          </li>
          <li id="p8k">
            <p>to make sure elements like pictures are the right size.  Elements</p>
          </li>
          <li id="TQL">
            <p>should go in order of visual weight</p>
          </li>
          <li id="69_">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="B6A">
            <p>If you have a series of elements in a row or column, and some are more</p>
          </li>
          <li id="PlD">
            <p>visually heavy than others (two buttons and three links, for example),</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ZZW">
            <p>you should arrange them like a triangle. The visually heaviest element</p>
          </li>
          <li id="MZ9">
            <p>should go first, and the least heavy element last, in order. One</p>
          </li>
          <li id="oUc">
            <p>caveat is that the visually heaviest element should be on the outside</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wBi">
            <p>edge. If your elements are against the right edge of the design, for</p>
          </li>
          <li id="G-B">
            <p>example, the heaviest element should be against the right</p>
          </li>
          <li id="USr">
            <p>edge. Elements that go in size or weight order look more satisfying.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="MyG">
            <p>If you use a horizontal grid, use 12 columns</p>
          </li>
          <li id="EhO">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="_aN">
            <p>If you’re going to break your design up into vertical columns, use 12</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Brv">
            <p>columns. A 12 column grid can be broken up into 1 column, 2 columns, 3</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Klr">
            <p>columns, and 4 columns, so it gives you a lot of flexibility.  Spacing</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4x5">
            <p>should go between points of high contrast</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WRn">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="lTf">
            <p>When you’re measuring out space between elements in a design—for</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_Qf">
            <p>example if you want 100px of vertical space between blocks of content</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2BX">
            <p>on a landing page—the spacing should be from one point of high</p>
          </li>
          <li id="B60">
            <p>contrast to the next. This is because our eyes find the edges of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="irv">
            <p>elements based on contrast, so we expect the spacing to run between</p>
          </li>
          <li id="YrR">
            <p>points of contrast.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Cfg">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="s0E">
            <p>A white background with black paragraphs of text means that the points</p>
          </li>
          <li id="lrt">
            <p>of contrast will be the end of one paragraph and the start of the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Z-T">
            <p>next. But if you put a black background behind one white paragraph,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="xyA">
            <p>the spacing should run from the end of one paragraph to the start of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="scS">
            <p>the black background, then again from the start of the black</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Sgq">
            <p>background to the start of the paragraph.  Closer elements should be</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ouW">
            <p>lighter</p>
          </li>
          <li id="D3N">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="7fo">
            <p>As elements on the screen get closer to the user, they should get</p>
          </li>
          <li id="BXM">
            <p>lighter. This applies to both light and dark mode UIs, because it</p>
          </li>
          <li id="L1n">
            <p>matches how the real world works.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="po_">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="wVk">
            <p>In the example below, the left side is incorrect and the right side is</p>
          </li>
          <li id="OM2">
            <p>correct.  Make drop shadow blur values double their distance values</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Iwp">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Va9">
            <p>e.g. If you create a shadow which extends 4 pixels on the Y axis, use</p>
          </li>
          <li id="DZJ">
            <p>a blur value of 8 pixels. As the element gets “closer” to the viewer,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="DFW">
            <p>it’s a good idea to also lower the opacity of the shadow. This looks</p>
          </li>
          <li id="-_n">
            <p>good because as elements move closer to the light source, their</p>
          </li>
          <li id="m1W">
            <p>shadows get more blurry.  Put simple on complex or complex on simple</p>
          </li>
          <li id="z5b">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="SrO">
            <p>A complex background (e.g. a colourful gradient fill) works best if</p>
          </li>
          <li id="34a">
            <p>the foreground (e.g. text) is simple. And a complex foreground element</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5RK">
            <p>is best on a simple background. You can put simple on simple, but it</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mNA">
            <p>tends to look plain. Complex on complex should be avoided because it’s</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2nI">
            <p>hard to pull off and will add visual clutter.  Keep container colours</p>
          </li>
          <li id="EN3">
            <p>within brightness limits</p>
          </li>
          <li id="r3R">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="RwV">
            <p>The brightness difference between background and container should be</p>
          </li>
          <li id="U1k">
            <p>within 12% for dark interfaces, and 7% for light interfaces. These</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nNp">
            <p>percentages refer to the brightness value in the HSB colour system.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wbd">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="nL0">
            <p>This is based on a study of about 100 well-designed websites where I</p>
          </li>
          <li id="fHb">
            <p>checked the brightness of containers against the backgrounds.  Make</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zHT">
            <p>outer padding the same or more than inner padding</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Umb">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Jsu">
            <p>In containers, inner padding is the space between elements inside the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="hKF">
            <p>container. Outer padding is the space between the elements and the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Q6q">
            <p>edges of the container. This outer padding should be the same or more</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ClA">
            <p>than the inner padding. Elements that are more related should be</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4Ru">
            <p>closer together. The elements inside a container are more related to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="6-L">
            <p>each other than they are to the container itself.  Keep body text at</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Lww">
            <p>16px or above</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ffs">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="50G">
            <p>16px is the default text size in most browsers. Text below this size</p>
          </li>
          <li id="s_f">
            <p>gets harder to read, so it’s safest to avoid it for body text. The</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ABy">
            <p>higher you go beyond 16px, the easier the text is to read.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Exu">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="0iE">
            <p>If you are writing code yourself, use whatever equivalent of pixels</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Sc-">
            <p>you prefer.  Use a line length around 70 characters</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Jz2">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="jck">
            <p>It doesn’t matter too much if your line length is 60 or 80 characters,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="sYY">
            <p>but go too far either side of that and you might run into subtle</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WHP">
            <p>readability issues.  Make horizontal padding twice the vertical</p>
          </li>
          <li id="xiu">
            <p>padding in buttons</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tY6">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="4G5">
            <p>The standard button pattern is wider than it is tall. If you want</p>
          </li>
          <li id="j0k">
            <p>people to recognise an element as a button, it’s a good idea to follow</p>
          </li>
          <li id="GC0">
            <p>the pattern. In the example below, the padding above and below the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="bAX">
            <p>label is 30px, and the padding to the left and right is 60px.  Use two</p>
          </li>
          <li id="0hg">
            <p>typefaces at most</p>
          </li>
          <li id="B7Q">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="_bB">
            <p>A second typeface is an opportunity to reinforce the concept behind a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Jat">
            <p>design. It also helps add some variety to a design. It’s rarely</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5S2">
            <p>necessary to use more than two, and it might make the design feel</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7tl">
            <p>visually confused.  Nest corners properly</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3KS">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Hzs">
            <p>Sometimes you will have two or more rounded corners nested</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Tbm">
            <p>together. If you want them to look right, set the inside corner radius</p>
          </li>
          <li id="dcm">
            <p>to the outside corner radius, minus the distance between the two. In</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ec_">
            <p>the example below the outside radius is 30px, and there is a 20px gap,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mIz">
            <p>so the inside corner radius is 10px.  Don’t put two hard divides next</p>
          </li>
          <li id="J0O">
            <p>to each other</p>
          </li>
          <li id="S0G">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="ju-">
            <p>Background transitions, container edges, and dividing lines will</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Gjw">
            <p>create hard visual divides. You should not have two or more hard</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Bhx">
            <p>divides next to each other. You can see this marked in red in the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Bw6">
            <p>example below. More than one hard divide creates visual clutter and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="pWQ">
            <p>catches the eye. In the example I have removed the background</p>
          </li>
          <li id="7uS">
            <p>transition so that the hard divide only comes from the container edge.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>

        <p>Writing about writing</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="Va">
            <p>It seems to me that writing is a therapeutic endeavour.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="5Z">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="ae">
            <p>With writing we can take a step back to analyse our thoughts and our thought processes.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Qp">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="LP">
            <p>It is meta-thinking.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="4MX-">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="PC">
            <p>This form of private journal is very helpful, I believe.</p>
          </li>

      <li id="kYG">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="7Am">
        <p>Photographs of Agony</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="hjp">
            <p>The possible contradictions of the war photograph now become</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3bD">
            <p>apparent. It is generally assumed that its purpose is to awaken</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Q8">
            <p>concern. The most extreme examples - as in most of McCullin's work -</p>
          </li>
          <li id="DM">
            <p>show moments of agony in order to exort the maximum concern. Such</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WM">
            <p>moments, whether photographed or not, are discontinuous with all other</p>
          </li>
          <li id="0n">
            <p>moments. They exist by themselves. But the reader who has been</p>
          </li>
          <li id="93">
            <p>arrested by the photograph may tend to feel this discontinuity as his</p>
          </li>
          <li id="vl">
            <p>own personal moral inadeuqecy. *And as soon as this happens even his*</p>
          </li>
          <li id="lj">
            <p>*sense of shock is dispersed*: his own moral inadequacy as being only</p>
          </li>
          <li id="q4">
            <p>too familiar, or else he thinks of performing a kind of penance - of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="kf">
            <p>which the purest example would be to make a contribution to Oxfam or</p>
          </li>
          <li id="vp">
            <p>Unicef. In both cases, the issue of the war which has caused that</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_BS">
            <p>moment is effectively depoliticised. The picture becomes evidence of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="KE">
            <p>the gerneal human condition. It accueses nobody and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2a">
            <p>everybody. Confrontation with a photographed moment of agony can mask</p>
          </li>
          <li id="A7">
            <p>a far more extensive and urgent confrontation. Usually the wars which</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Pk">
            <p>we are shown are being fought directlly or indirectly in 'our'</p>
          </li>
          <li id="y5">
            <p>name. What we are sshown horrifies us. The next step should be for us</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wJ">
            <p>to confront our own lack of political freedom. In the political</p>
          </li>
          <li id="rj">
            <p>systems as they exist, we have no legal opportunity of effectively</p>
          </li>
          <li id="6q">
            <p>influencing the conduct of wars waged in our name. To realise this and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="B6">
            <p>to act accordingly is the only effective way of responding to what the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="t0">
            <p>photograph shows. Yet the double violence of the photographed moment</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8C">
            <p>actually works against this realisation. That is why they can be</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Od">
            <p>published with impunity.</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="wF8">

      <li id="tGg">
        <p>Invisible Cities</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="CBR">
            <p>"You reach a moment in life when, among the people you have known, the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Ff">
            <p>dead outnumber the living. And the mind refuses to accept more faces,</p>
          </li>
          <li id="PZ">
            <p>more expressions: on every new face you encounter, it prints the old</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Mb">
            <p>forms, for each one it finds the most suitable mask."</p>
          </li>
        </ul>

      <li id="4-C">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="aP7">
        <p>Paper on boredom</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="Tar">
            <p>Heidegger’s (1929/30) lectures on The Fundamental Concepts of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="j7">
            <p>Metaphysics</p>
          </li>
          <li id="dB">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="gu">
            <p>Heidegger saw that through profound boredom, we can see the world</p>
          </li>
          <li id="tW">
            <p>anew, question what makes life meaningful to us, and connect these</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Z-">
            <p>interests to a me</p>
          </li>
          <li id="FW">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="_J">
            <p>As Heidegger (1995: 80) notes, boredom bears ‘an almost obvious</p>
          </li>
          <li id="iU">
            <p>relation to time, a way in which we stand with respect to time, a</p>
          </li>
          <li id="aa">
            <p>feeling of time’.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wP">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Qi">
            <p>Murphy, S., Hill, T., McDonagh, P., &amp; Flaherty, A. (2022). Mundane</p>
          </li>
          <li id="VX">
            <p>emotions: Losing yourself in boredom, time and technology. Marketing</p>
          </li>
          <li id="yB">
            <p>Theory, 0(0). &lt;https://doi.org/10.1177/14705931221138617&gt;</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="3h2">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="1JQ">
        <p>Conversion between reader and writer</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="Tfi">
            <p>"In a conversation that a reader has with a book in the privacy of his</p>
          </li>
          <li id="JA">
            <p>own study, there is nothing to prevent the reader from seeming to win</p>
          </li>
          <li id="GS">
            <p>the argument. He can dominate the situation. The author is not there</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Mx">
            <p>to defend himself. If all he wants is the empty satisfaction of</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3k">
            <p>seeming to show the author up, the reader can get it readily. He</p>
          </li>
          <li id="GN">
            <p>scarecly has to read the book through to get it. Glancing at the first</p>
          </li>
          <li id="X3">
            <p>few pages will suffice. But if he realises that the only profit in</p>
          </li>
          <li id="oV">
            <p>conversation, with living or deadteachers, is what one can learn from</p>
          </li>
          <li id="U3">
            <p>them, if he realises that you win only by gaining knowledge, not by</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nT">
            <p>knocking theirother fellow down, he may see the futility of mere</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2j">
            <p>contentiousness."</p>
          </li>
          <li id="lX">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Lf">
            <p>Mortimer J. Adler &amp;#x2013; How to Read a Book: The Classic Guide to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Lx">
            <p>Intelligent Reading</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="sVo">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="6iG">
        <p>Weaponising the admission of a mistake</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="GfB">
            <p>I think there is a tendency to weaponise another's mistake.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="64">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="hnW">
            <p>If someone admits to being mistaken, or for holding a compromised</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WP">
            <p>viewpoint, and then later admits to doing so, such an admission should</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9W">
            <p>not be held against them in future.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="v_">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="6q8">
            <p>This is because it risks spawning a viscious and self-perpetuating</p>
          </li>
          <li id="c1">
            <p>loop, whereby the instigator of the admission becomes less likely to</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9X">
            <p>concede in future arguments by default because it might be held</p>
          </li>
          <li id="mg">
            <p>against them for doing so.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ve">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="3p">
            <p>This can be circumvented easily via avoidance of focus on the current</p>
          </li>
          <li id="1cs">
            <p>argument. Avoid drawing attention to any past debates where either</p>
          </li>
          <li id="94v">
            <p>party was wrong or right. i.e. avoid the use of "but you said X and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="im">
            <p>the outcome was Y, I told you so."</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="4iD">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="yp6">
        <p>Phoenix applications are one giant function </p>
        <ul>
          <li id="gxL">
            <p>I think this aligns with the *functional* heart and nature of Elixir</p>
          </li>
          <li id="_U">
            <p>programs. The Phoenix framework is essentially one giant function that</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WH">
            <p>starts at `%Plug.Conn{}` and is then manipulated by additional plugs.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="B3">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Vu">
            <p>Every plug that comes into contact with this initial "connection"</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Mz">
            <p>plug, returns it in some updated state. It is a really nice pipeline</p>
          </li>
          <li id="CF">
            <p>paradigm.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="it">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="ZA">
            <p>Another way to think about this is a *production line* in a factory. The</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3f">
            <p>request works its way down the conveyor belt and is modified along the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="9x">
            <p>way to result in some arbitrary response (that differs from the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="x5">
            <p>request in some way).</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Tf">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="HI">
            <p>The example given in the book on LiveView is the following:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3r">
            <p/>
            <ul>
              <li id="aX">
                <p>connection_from_request</p>
                <ul>
                  <li id="3_">
                    <p>|&gt; endpoint</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="w5">
                    <p>|&gt; router</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="Gi">
                    <p>|&gt; custom_application</p>
                  </li>
                  <li id="rD">
                    <p/>
                  </li>
                </ul>
              </li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li id="Dk">
            <p>Associated reading: [Anatomy of a Phoenix app](20221218T174402--anatomy-of-a-phoenix-app.md), [Notes on Programming</p>
          </li>
          <li id="L3">
            <p>Phoenix LiveView](20221218T161250--notes-on-programming-phoenix-liveview.md)</p>
          </li>
          <li id="8sw">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="_aF">
            <p>The interesting thing about this is you can use the game "Factorio" as</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Er4">
            <p>an almost 1-1 mapping of the system.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="oRB">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="sx0">
            <p>This is how you know that Phoenix is a well-designed framework.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ZiT">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="sB3">
            <p>Conveyor belts = pipeline</p>
          </li>
          <li id="C36">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="RS3">
            <p>Plugs = assemblers or assembly machines</p>
          </li>
          <li id="2vN">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="cyL">
            <p>Router = some advance splitter or load balancer</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="3dZ">
        <p/>
      </li>
      <li id="FkI">
        <p>Anatomy of a phoenix app</p>
        <ul>
          <li id="cdd">
            <p># Table of Contents</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Z1">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="EE">
            <p>1.  [Changesets](#org6b655e5)</p>
          </li>
          <li id="cI">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="ht">
            <p>Phoenix apps foster a very idiomatic and pragmatic layout.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="ZT">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="6g">
            <p>All assets required for the project are stored in an `assets` directory.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zx">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="gd">
            <p>Any web-based code is stored inside `lib/proj_web` .</p>
          </li>
          <li id="jo">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="XS">
            <p>All core business logic and backend code is housed inside `lib/proj`.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="isD">
            <p>(where `proj` is the name of the project)</p>
          </li>
          <li id="DD">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="qa">
            <p>Interestingly, the `context` is referred to as the `api` for the</p>
          </li>
          <li id="l1">
            <p>application. I'm not entirely sure how this analogy fits together just</p>
          </li>
          <li id="3D">
            <p>yet.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="hD">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="hM">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="uh">
            <p>&lt;a id="org6b655e5"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;</p>
          </li>
          <li id="S2">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="UF">
            <p># Changesets</p>
          </li>
          <li id="By">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Ox">
            <p>Inside a changeset, the use of `cast` is effectively a filter for fields.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="oM">
            <p>Only fields included in the `cast` are allowed, e.g.:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="WT">
            <p/>
            <ul>
              <li id="wR">
                <p>|&gt; cast(attrs, [:name])</p>
              </li>
              <li id="L_">
                <p/>
              </li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li id="VD">
            <p>Only the `:name` attribute is accepted by the above cahngeset.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Cd">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="tH">
            <p>Cast also converts the attributes into the `type` that is expected by</p>
          </li>
          <li id="gp">
            <p>the schema.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="zB">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="jn">
            <p>`push_redirect` is used as a server-side push (I believe this to be</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Fi">
            <p>somewhat equivalent to Next. `live_redirect` is the client-side version</p>
          </li>
          <li id="O4">
            <p>of this function.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="qP">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="bR">
            <p>I do wonder what the logic behind this naming is?</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Pe">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="Ts">
            <p>Surely `server_redirect` and `client_redirect` are preferable?</p>
          </li>
          <li id="oW4">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="KA">
            <p>It is possible to include references in the generation step:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="wC">
            <p/>
            <ul>
              <li id="k5">
                <p>mix phx.gen.context Survey Demographic demographics gender:string \</p>
              </li>
              <li id="uC">
                <p>year_of_birth:integer user_id:references:users:unique</p>
              </li>
              <li id="m7">
                <p/>
              </li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li id="Sf">
            <p>And similarly with:</p>
          </li>
          <li id="AW">
            <p/>
            <ul>
              <li id="-D">
                <p>mix phx.gen.context Survey Rating ratings stars:integer \</p>
              </li>
              <li id="vI">
                <p>user_id:references:users product_id:references:products</p>
              </li>
              <li id="R6">
                <p/>
              </li>
            </ul>
          </li>
          <li id="Dw">
            <p>&gt; Ecto query composition, as you already know, is certain and</p>
          </li>
          <li id="Nj">
            <p>&gt; predictable. It belongs in your application’s core.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="bt">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="0g">
            <p>&gt; Remember, Ecto is explicit. If you want it to load relationships, you</p>
          </li>
          <li id="IC">
            <p>&gt; need to ask for them.</p>
          </li>
          <li id="MN">
            <p/>
          </li>
          <li id="qd">
            <p>&gt; A function component is a function that takes in an assigns argument</p>
          </li>
          <li id="nh">
            <p>&gt; and returns a HEEx template</p>
          </li>
        </ul>
      </li>
      <li id="8s4">
        <p/>
